related links\">Related links</a>\n  </div>\n  <div class=\"reveal-content\">\n    <div class=\"article-meta\"> \n      <ul>\n      \t<li><a href=\"http://adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2019ApJ...876..107P\" target=\"_blank\">NASA ADS Record <span class=\"icon-newtab\"></span></a></li>\n      \t<li><a href=\"http://simbad.u-strasbg.fr/simbad/sim-ref?querymethod=bib&simbo=on&bibcode=2019ApJ...876..107P\" target=\"_blank\">Simbad Objects <span class=\"icon-newtab\"></span></a></li>\n      \t<li><a href=\"/journal/0004-637X/page/About%20Related%20Links\" target=\"_blank\">About Related Links</a></li>\n      \t</ul>\n    </div>\n  </div>\n</div>\n<!-- Start info bottom -->\n    <!-- End info bottom -->\n<!-- Start article full text -->\n\t<div xmlns:book=\"http://api.iop.org/Book/1.0/\" itemprop=\"articleBody\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-full-text article-text\">\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjab164as1\" name=\"apjab164as1\">1.\u00a0Introduction</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>The theory to enable the use of a gravitationally lensed supernova (SN) resolved into multiple images as a cosmological tool was developed in the seminal work of Refsdal (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib43\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib43\">1964</a>). As the light for each of the multiple images follows a different path through the expanding universe and through the lensing potential, the SN images appear delayed by hours (for galaxy-scale lenses) or years (for cluster-scale lenses). These time delays are sensitive to various cosmological parameters, enabling new measurements of the Hubble Constant <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> and the dark-energy equation of state (Linder <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib35\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib35\">2011</a>; Treu &amp; Marshall <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib56\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib56\">2016</a>). Time delay cosmography has been employed extremely successfully for decades using multiply imaged quasars (e.g., Vuissoz et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib57\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib57\">2008</a>; Suyu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib53\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib53\">2010</a>; Tewes et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib55\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib55\">2013b</a>; Bonvin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib9\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib9\">2017</a>; Birrer et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib6\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib6\">2018</a>), but using gravitationally lensed SNe with multiple images (hereafter glSNe) can extend and enhance this method for several reasons.</p><p>glSNe have a strong luminosity peak and occur on short timescales, enabling relatively simple and accurate time delay measurements (e.g., Woosley et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib61\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib61\">2007</a>; Sanders et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib49\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib49\">2015</a>). In addition, the intrinsic luminosities of Type Ia and certain Type II SNe can be inferred independently of lensing (Phillips <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib40\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib40\">1993</a>; Hamuy &amp; Pinto <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib21\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib21\">2002</a>; Kasen &amp; Woosley <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib27\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib27\">2009</a>; Poznanski et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib42\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib42\">2009</a>), which helps to determine the absolute lensing magnifications. This piece of information, not available for lensed quasars, provides an independent check on the lens model and the characterization of the line of sight, thus helping to break the mass-sheet degeneracy (e.g., Holz <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib22\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib22\">2001</a>; Rodney et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib46\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib46\">2015</a>), which will otherwise introduce an additional uncertainty on <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> (Kolatt &amp; Bartelmann <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib32\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib32\">1998</a>; Xu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib62\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib62\">2016</a>).</p><p>The first multiply imaged core-collapse (SN Refsdal: Kelly et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib29\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib29\">2015</a>) and Type Ia (SN iPTF16geu: Goobar et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib17\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib17\">2016</a>) SNe have been discovered in just the past few years. However, it is anticipated that the next generation of telescopes, particularly the Large Synoptic Survey Telescope (LSST) and the <em>Wide-field Infrared Survey Telescope</em> (<em>WFIRST</em>), will provide hundreds to thousands of Type Ia and CC glSNe observations over the next decade (Oguri &amp; Marshall <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib38\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib38\">2010</a>; Goldstein &amp; Nugent <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib14\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib14\">2017</a>). With this enormous volume of observed glSNe, it will become impractical to employ such individual treatments of time delay measurements seen in Rodney et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib47\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib47\">2016</a>) and Goobar et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib17\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib17\">2016</a>). Incidentally, the time delay measurement techniques employed in these cases ignored the effects of \"microlensing\", whereby each SN image is separately varied by lensing effects from stars in the lens plane (e.g., Bagherpour et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib2\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib2\">2006</a>; Dobler &amp; Keeton <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib10\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib10\">2006</a>; Foxley-Marrable et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib12\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib12\">2018</a>). This stellar-scale lensing operates at microarcsecond scales and can introduce uncertainty in the time delay measurement of ~4%, or ~2.4 days for a time delay of 60 days. (Goldstein et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib16\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib16\">2018b</a>).</p><p>In this work, we have developed an open-source software package, written in Python, called <em>Supernova Time Delays</em> (<em>SNTD</em>).<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjab164afn1\">1</a></sup>\n The SNTD package is capable of making accurate time delay measurements for glSNe of all types while including treatments of microlensing and can immediately produce accurate simulations for wide-field time-domain surveys such as LSST and <em>WFIRST</em>. Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as2\">2</a> describes the simulation toolkit in SNTD including updated light-curve models, the treatment of microlensing, and examples of SNTD-generated light curves using hypothetical lensed SN case studies. Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as3\">3</a> describes the time delay measurement capabilities of SNTD. This section uses the case-study lensing systems from Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as2\">2</a> to assess the accuracy and precision of these measurements, and includes a comparison of time delay measurement accuracy for before-peak and after-peak SN detections.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjab164as2\" name=\"apjab164as2\">2.\u00a0The SNTD Simulation Toolkit</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>The simulation toolkit within SNTD allows a user to simulate realistic glSN observations with a variety of SN and lensing properties for various SN classifications. This capability can be used for design and optimization of surveys and follow-up campaigns. The software used inside SNTD for generating light curves and the ways in which it has been modified for use with glSNe are described in Sections <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as2-1\">2.1</a> and <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as2-2\">2.2</a>. Microlensing is described in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as2-3\">2.3</a>, both how it can be simulated and its inclusion in light-curve simulations. In Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as2-4\">2.4</a>, we use a representative lensed system to create simulated glSN observations, with microlensing included, to be analyzed for time delays in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as3\">3</a>.</p><h3 id=\"apjab164as2-1\" name=\"apjab164as2-1\">2.1.\u00a0SNCosmo and Current Light-curve Templates</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>As discussed in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as1\">1</a>, SNe are an advantageous tool for time delay cosmography due to their light-curve shape and timescales. Unlike the stochastic and heterogeneous light curves of AGNs, we have well-defined spectrophotometric time-series to describe a variety of SN types, with coverage from the ultraviolet (UV) to the near-infrared (near-IR). Within SNTD, these spectrophotometric time-series are used to fit observed glSN light curves, using the SNCosmo<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjab164afn2\">2</a></sup>\n python toolkit (Barbary <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib3\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib3\">2014</a>). SNCosmo has dozens of empirically defined time-series to describe the evolution of SNe Ia, Ib, Ic, II-P, II-L, and IIn, as well as the parametric SALT2 model for SNe Ia (see Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjab164at2\">2</a> in the <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164aapp1\">Appendix</a>). These SNCosmo models have coverage in the UV to optical wavelength range and were extended by Pierel et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib41\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib41\">2018</a>) to include more of the UV and near-IR.</p><p>SNCosmo is a powerful tool for supernova cosmology, enabling users to fit existing models to observed data at any redshift while considering the effects dust extinction from any point along the line of sight. The toolkit is also capable of using any of the spectrophotometric time-series and fitting capabilities to produce realistic simulations of observed light curves. However, the toolkit is not designed for the analysis of lensed SNe, having no capabilities for handling macrolensing, microlensing, or glSNe. SNCosmo is also not equipped for the specific case of SN Refsdal, the first glSN discovered, which happened to be an unusual SN that cannot be fully described by existing SN classification templates (e.g., Arnett et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib1\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib1\">1989</a>; Kelly et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib28\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib28\">2016</a>).</p></div><h3 id=\"apjab164as2-2\" name=\"apjab164as2-2\">2.2.\u00a0Extending SNCosmo</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>As SNCosmo is already widely used by the SN research community, we have opted to extend the package to provide optimized glSN capabilities that work within SNTD, as opposed to creating an entirely new framework. The updates made are as follows:</p><p><em>Parametric light-curve model</em>: while not uniquely useful to studies of glSNe, an important addition to the capabilities of SNCosmo is the inclusion of a parameterized model that operates within the framework of the existing package. This parameterized model allows a user to fit photometric data without knowledge of the SN classification or redshift, enabling time delay measurements in the cases where there is no spectroscopic follow-up or the data are not well fit with common light-curve templates (e.g., Rodney et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib47\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib47\">2016</a>).</p><p>A common practice for fitting photometric data with no underlying light-curve template involves the use of splines or other flexible functions (i.e., Tewes et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib54\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib54\">2013a</a>), but the simplicity of SN light curves enables the use of a parameterized model that has fewer free parameters. The parameterized light-curve model defined by Bazin et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib4\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib4\">2009</a>) is implemented in SNCosmo for use in SNTD, which calculates flux as a function of time in such a way as to be general enough to fit any light-curve shape. While useful for fitting light curves in certain situations, this parameterized model describes only the shape of an SN light curve; it does not have any information about the SN SED, so it cannot leverage information about the color of an observed SN. The Bazin et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib4\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib4\">2009</a>) model is defined according to</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjab164aeqn1\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/876/2/107/Full/apjab164aeqn1.gif\" alt=\"Equation (1)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&F(t)=A\\displaystyle \\frac{{e}^{-(t-{t}_{0})/{\\tau }_{\\mathrm{fall}}}}{1+{e}^{-(t-{t}_{0})/{\\tau }_{\\mathrm{rise}}}}+B,\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t1\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/876/2/107/Full/apjab164aieqn1.gif\" alt=\"${\\tau }_{\\mathrm{rise}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\tau }_{\\mathrm{rise}}</script></span></span> and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/876/2/107/Full/apjab164aieqn2.gif\" alt=\"${\\tau }_{\\mathrm{fall}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\tau }_{\\mathrm{fall}}</script></span></span> characterize the relative rise and decline times of the light curve, while <em>A</em> and <em>B</em> are constants. By taking the derivative, it is apparent that the time of peak and peak flux are</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjab164aeqn2\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/876/2/107/Full/apjab164aeqn2.gif\" alt=\"Equation (2)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{t}_{\\max }={t}_{0}+{\\tau }_{\\mathrm{rise}}\\mathrm{ln}\\left(\\displaystyle \\frac{{\\tau }_{\\mathrm{fall}}}{{\\tau }_{\\mathrm{rise}}}\\right)\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t2\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>\n</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjab164aeqn3\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/876/2/107/Full/apjab164aeqn3.gif\" alt=\"Equation (3)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&f({t}_{\\max })={{Ax}}^{x}{\\left(1-x\\right)}^{1-x}+B;x=\\displaystyle \\frac{{\\tau }_{\\mathrm{rise}}}{{\\tau }_{\\mathrm{fall}}}.\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t3\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>While this model is extremely flexible, it still mimics the smooth rise and fall of a general SN light curve, which helps to keep a light-curve fit relatively insensitive to the effects of sharp microlensing features.</p><p><em>Microlensing:</em> we describe the phenomenon of microlensing, as well as SNTD's method for simulating it, in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as2-3\">2.3</a>. Once the simulation is complete however, the effect can be simply described by a time-evolved \"magnification curve\". This curve is added to an SN light curve as an SNCosmo \"propagation effect\", in the same way as dust extinction is handled. These effects are simply models of how intervening structures affect a spectrum, as well as the associated light curve. The microlensing propagation effect causes time-dependent flux-scaling when applied to the simulated light-curve points. Each image of a glSN will be along a different line of sight, subjecting it to unique microlensing effects. Therefore, during simulations, a separate microlensing effect is applied to each light curve as it is generated (See Sections <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as2-3\">2.3</a> and <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as2-4\">2.4</a>).</p><p><em>Multiply imaged supernovae:</em> knowing that observations of glSNe stem from a single light curve is extremely valuable in measuring time delays (see Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as3\">3</a>). As SNCosmo has no structures for handling this phenomenon, SNTD contains new objects capable of maintaining maximum information about the SN and each of its images for use in time delay measurements. All components of the new objects operate within the current SNCosmo framework so that, for example, each SN image has a usable light curve within existing SNCosmo functions.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjab164as2-3\" name=\"apjab164as2-3\">2.3.\u00a0Microlensing</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The phenomenon of microlensing occurs when light rays from the expanding photosphere of an SN pass within the lensing potential of a set of stars in the lens plane, which have Einstein radii on the order of microarcseconds (Dobler &amp; Keeton <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib10\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib10\">2006</a>). This causes fluctuations in the light curve of ~0.2 to &gt;0.5 mag on timescales of weeks to months, which can make it difficult to obtain an accurate time delay measurement without accounting for microlensing in certain lens configurations (Dobler &amp; Keeton <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib10\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib10\">2006</a>; Foxley-Marrable et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib12\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib12\">2018</a>; Goldstein et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib16\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib16\">2018b</a>; Bonvin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib7\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib7\">2019</a>).</p><p>To simulate the effects of microlensing, we employ the <em>microlens</em> inverse ray-tracing code (Wambsganss <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib58\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib58\">1999</a>). The <em>microlens</em> code defines random realizations of stars modeled as point-masses in the lens plane according to two parameters: <em>f</em><sub>*</sub> and <em>q</em>. The parameter <em>f</em><sub>*</sub> defines the relative fraction of convergence (<em>\u03ba</em>) from discrete sources such as stars compared to smooth sources such as dark matter. The parameter <em>q</em> is the stellar mass ratio (<em>m</em><sub>min</sub>/<em>m</em><sub>max</sub>) of the stellar mass function. The microlensing magnification distributions produced by <em>microlens</em> require a further two parameters, namely the shear due to the Singular Isothermal Ellipsoid and external potentials (<em>\u03b3</em>), and the local convergence (<em>\u03ba</em>). The convergence causes a focusing of light rays, leading to an isotropic magnification of a source, while the shear introduces an anisotropy that distorts the shape of the magnified source (Narayan &amp; Bartelmann <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib36\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib36\">1996</a>).</p><p>Once the microcaustic field is generated by the <em>microlens</em> code for a particular set of parameters, it must be convolved with a model for the expanding photosphere of an SN. By default, we create a model for an expanding SN photosphere as flat-disk achromatic Gaussian brightness distribution. This photospheric model is a simplifying assumption also made in previous microlensing analyses (e.g., Foxley-Marrable et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib12\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib12\">2018</a>), which other work has shown to be sufficiently accurate up to an ignored \"microlensing time delay\" on the order of 0.1 days (Bonvin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib7\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib7\">2019</a>). The microcaustic field defines a grid of positive and negative magnifications in the source plane. SNTD can produce a magnification curve\u2014describing the effect of any given microlensing realization with time\u2014by propagating the expanding photospheric model through the microcaustic field (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab164af1\">1</a>). As this default model is achromatic, the resulting magnification curve will be a function of only time. In principle, SNTD is capable of replacing this simple model with a two-dimensional projected specific intensity profile, like that of Goldstein et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib16\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib16\">2018b</a>). This method would better represent an SN atmosphere, which emits light differently as a function of radius from the center (e.g., Kasen <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib26\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib26\">2006</a>) in such a way that a time delay effect can also be observed (e.g., Bonvin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib7\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib7\">2019</a>). This substitution could be made by a user, or may perhaps be the default in a future iteration of SNTD. The SN image can be placed randomly at any position in the microcaustic field, so that one can explore the variety of microlensing effects that may arise for a given set of lensing parameters (<em>\u03ba</em>, <em>\u03b3</em>, <em>f</em><sub>*</sub>, <em>q</em>). The size of each microcaustic field projected onto the source plane is 10<em>R</em><sub>E</sub> on a side, where <em>R</em><sub>E</sub> is the Einstein radius of a typical deflector of mass <em>M</em> in the lens plane:</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjab164aeqn4\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/876/2/107/Full/apjab164aeqn4.gif\" alt=\"Equation (4)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{R}_{{\\rm{E}}}=\\sqrt{\\displaystyle \\frac{4{GM}}{{c}^{2}}\\displaystyle \\frac{{D}_{\\mathrm{ls}}{D}_{{\\rm{s}}}}{{D}_{{\\rm{l}}}}},\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t4\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where <em>D</em><sub>s</sub>, <em>D</em><sub>l</sub>, and <em>D</em><sub>ls</sub> define the angular diameter distances to the source, lens, and between the lens and source, respectively.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjab164af1\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjab164af1\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/876/2/107/Full/apjab164af1_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 1.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 1.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/876/2/107/Full/apjab164af1_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 1.</strong>\u00a0Microlensing microcaustic realization using SNTD, where caustics that increase magnification are blue, and caustics that decrease magnification are red. In this case, the lensing parameters described in Section <a class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as2-3\">2.3</a> are set to (<em>\u03ba</em>, <em>\u03b3</em>, <em>f</em><sub>*</sub>, <em>q</em>)\u00a0=\u00a0(0.31, 0.61, 0.5, 0.2) and the photospheric velocity is set to <span class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/876/2/107/Full/apjab164aieqn3.gif\" alt=\"${10}^{4}\\,\\mathrm{km}\\,{{\\rm{s}}}^{-1}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{10}^{4}\\,\\mathrm{km}\\,{{\\rm{s}}}^{-1}</script></span></span>, slightly below the average peak photospheric velocity for SNe Ia (Zheng et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib63\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib63\">2018</a>). This photospheric velocity is nevertheless much larger than the peculiar velocities and proper motions in the lens and source planes, so we approximate the centroid of a supernova as constant with only the radius of the supernova changing with time (Foxley-Marrable et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib12\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib12\">2018</a>).</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjab164af1\" href=\"/0004-637X/876/2/107/downloadFigure/figure/apjab164af1\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjab164af1\" href=\"/0004-637X/876/2/107/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjab164af1\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div><h3 id=\"apjab164as2-4\" name=\"apjab164as2-4\">2.4.\u00a0SNTD Simulation Examples</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>In order to demonstrate the simulation and time delay measurement capabilities of SNTD, we created 2000 simulated glSNe using an imagined galaxy-scale strong-lensing system that is similar to the lenses described by Shu et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib50\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib50\">2018</a>). It should be noted that a true SN detection in such a galaxy would require excellent follow-up observations from space and/or large ground-based observatories to obtain sufficient data quality for time delay cosmography. This mock lensing galaxy is placed at redshift (<em>z</em><sub>lens</sub>) of 0.5, and the source galaxy is placed at a redshift (<em>z</em><sub>source</sub>) of 1.4. The overall magnification factor (<em>\u03bc</em>) for the leading image of an SN located at the light peak of the lensed galaxy is set to 5, and the relative time delay (\u0394<em>t</em>) between the two images is set to 60 days. The measurement of time delays for these mock lensed SNe is described in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as3\">3</a>.</p><p>Each simulated SN has an absolute magnitude in the <em>B</em> bandpass defined by a Gaussian luminosity function (see Tables <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjab164at4\">4</a> and <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjab164at5\">5</a> in the <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164aapp1\">Appendix</a>), a redshift equal to the host galaxy's <em>z</em><sub>source</sub>, and host galaxy extinction defined by the dust distributions used by Rodney et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib45\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib45\">2014</a>). Magnification and time delay factors are applied to each SN image, defined by the lensing galaxy's <em>\u03bc</em> and \u0394<em>t</em> described above, respectively.</p><p>A microcaustic field is randomly generated at a redshift equal to the lensing galaxy's <em>z</em><sub>lens</sub> described above, following the methodology described in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as2-3\">2.3</a> using lensing parameters representative of the lenses presented by Shu et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib50\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib50\">2018</a>). The resulting microlensing magnification curve is applied to each simulated light curve as an added propagation effect, causing positive and negative flux variations with time (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab164af1\">1</a>).</p><p>To simulate realistic light curves, the observational strategies presented by Shu et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib50\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib50\">2018</a>) are adopted, which describe a survey strategy that targets lensed galaxies with high star formation rates and prior knowledge of the lensing parameters. The photometric data are generated with observational parameters for the <em>Hubble Space Telescope</em> (<em>HST</em>) F125W and F160W filters and a fixed signal-to-noise ratio (S/N) curve based on the <em>HST</em> exposure time calculator. We simulate 1000 glSNe for which the SN is first detected before peak brightness, and another 1000 glSNe for which the first detection lands after peak brightness. With these two sets of simulations, we can ask by how much is the precision of an SN time delay measurement degraded when there is no detection before peak brightness. This will be an important consideration for the choice of cadence and depth in future surveys and also for the allocation of resources for follow-up observations.</p><p>An example of an observations table used by SNCosmo to realize a set of light curves is given in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjab164at3\">3</a> in the <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164aapp1\">Appendix</a>. SNTD simulates the multiple images of a single SN by using the same absolute magnitude and host galaxy dust parameters discussed above for each light curve as they are inherent to the SN, then scaling the simulated flux measurements and shifting the time axis of the trailing image by the prescribed time delay and magnification parameters. Finally, the microlensing magnification curve described above and observational noise are applied to the simulated observations, causing further variations in the flux with time.</p><p><em>Before peak detections:</em> we first generated light curves under the assumption that we were able to \"observe\" each leading SN image before its epoch of peak brightness. It is ideal to obtain this before-peak observation, so that the peak of the light curve is well-defined and more easily fit. An SN is simulated using the methods outlined above in this section, then a random epoch is chosen between the epoch at which the brightness of the SN reaches the detection threshold of the telescope, and the epoch of its peak brightness. This epoch defines the first observation of the leading image.</p><p>Once the SN is \"observed\", we assume that a set of non-disruptive follow-up observations are made by the <em>HST</em>. This necessitates a two-week gap between the first epoch and the second, and thereafter a five-day cadence is used to \"observe\" the SN until the trailing image is no longer detectable (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab164af2\">2</a>(A)). This process is repeated to create a mock catalog of 1000 realistic glSN light curves.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjab164af2\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjab164af2\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/876/2/107/Full/apjab164af2_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 2.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 2.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/876/2/107/Full/apjab164af2_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 2.</strong>\u00a0Example simulated glSN Ia from each of the case studies described in this section. In each panel, the solid black line is the underlying template used to generate the photometry before microlensing and observational noise are added, blue is used for the leading image, and red is used for the trailing image. The microlensing curves (see Section <a class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as2-3\">2.3</a>) added to the light curves are shown in the lower panels. The left panel displays the two images of an SN for which the leading image was detected before peak (A), and the right panel displays the two images of an SN for which the leading image was detected after peak (B).</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjab164af2\" href=\"/0004-637X/876/2/107/downloadFigure/figure/apjab164af2\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjab164af2\" href=\"/0004-637X/876/2/107/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjab164af2\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p><em>After peak detections:</em> The process of generating a realistic catalog of 1000 glSN light curves is repeated under the assumption that we only detect each leading SN image after its epoch of peak brightness. It is more difficult to identify the peak of a light curve, and therefore the relative time delay, without any before-peak observations due to the lack of a primary inflection point.</p><p>The light curves for the glSNe detected after peak are generated by taking the first observation to be a random epoch between the epoch of peak brightness and the epoch at which the brightness of the SN falls below the detection threshold of the telescope. This detection epoch simultaneously defines the first observation of the trailing image, which is nearly always before the peak in this case due to the relative time delay of 60 days. Then, we again assume that a set of non-disruptive follow-up observations are made by the <em>HST</em>. This results in a two-week gap between the first epoch and the second, and thereafter a five-day cadence is used to \"observe\" the SN until the trailing image is no longer detectable (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab164af2\">2</a>(B)).</p></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjab164as3\" name=\"apjab164as3\">3.\u00a0The SNTD Time Delay Measurement Toolkit</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We describe SNTD's treatment of microlensing in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as3-1\">3.1</a>, and then the various tools available to measure the time delays and relative magnifications of glSNe. The methods include parallel analysis (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as3-2\">3.2</a>), series analysis (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as3-3\">3.3</a>), and color curve analysis (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as3-4\">3.4</a>). Next, the simulated samples of glSNe created in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as2-4\">2.4</a> are used to compare these three time delay measurement techniques.</p><h3 id=\"apjab164as3-1\" name=\"apjab164as3-1\">3.1.\u00a0Accounting for the Effects of Microlensing</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Identifying and correcting for microlensing in a light curve is an extremely difficult problem, driven mainly by the stochasticity of the effect and the degeneracies present between the extrinsic and intrinsic model parameters. A pathological but plausible case of microlensing is capable of leaving the shape of a light curve relatively untouched, while shifting the peak in time (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab164af3\">3</a>). The effect of microlensing from a modeling perspective would be classified as low in this case, as the model performs well in fitting the data, but there is an added systematic error unaccounted for in the measurement of the time of peak that will propagate through to an error in the time delay.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjab164af3\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjab164af3\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/876/2/107/Full/apjab164af3_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 3.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 3.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/876/2/107/Full/apjab164af3_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 3.</strong>\u00a0Contrived case of microlensing where the shape of the light curve is untouched, but the time of peak is shifted by several days. First, a Type Ib supernova model produces a light curve with no microlensing added (blue). Next, a pathological but realistic microlensing curve (black) is applied to the non-lensed model. The resulting curve (orange) is identical to the non-lensed curve with the time of peak shifted (green). While this scenario is not likely, this idealistic case shows the extreme at which a microlensing curve can mimic a shift in the time of peak of a light curve, which would not be caught by any data-driven modeling process but contributes a systematic error to the time delay measurement.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjab164af3\" href=\"/0004-637X/876/2/107/downloadFigure/figure/apjab164af3\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjab164af3\" href=\"/0004-637X/876/2/107/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjab164af3\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>Therefore, we seek to better characterize the systematic error in the time delay measurement due to microlensing, which if unaccounted for would cause an underestimation of the time delay measurement uncertainty. When not in the microlensing regime described in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab164af3\">3</a>, fitting light curves for time delays without considering microlensing remains quite effective, due to the existence of templates for various SN classifications that reduce the flexibility of models. In these cases, the main deviations from the model should be due to microlensing effects, which are then visible in the residuals. However, simply fitting a curve to the residuals and attributing all of those deviations to microlensing is inadequate, because it will create a bias in your time delay measurement if your initial fit is poor, or may incorrectly attribute observational scatter to a microlensing effect (e.g., Tewes et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib54\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib54\">2013a</a>). Therefore, we avoid directly fitting for a microlensing spline and instead use Monte Carlo simulations of realistic microlensing magnification curves to characterize the additional error on the time delay measurement as follows:</p><ul class=\"clear-list wd-inline-list\"><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">1.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">A best-fit model for the intrinsic SN light curve is found by fitting observed light-curve data with a model that has no microlensing included (see Sections <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as3-2\">3.2</a>\u2013<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as3-4\">3.4</a>). This fit defines a measured time delay \u0394<em>t</em>.</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">2.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">Trends in the residuals of the best-fit model are identified using Gaussian Process Regression (GPR), which produces a posterior distribution of possible microlensing functions.</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">3.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">A representative sample of <em>N</em> (typically 100) microlensing curves is then extracted from the GPR posterior (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab164af4\">4</a>).</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">4.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">The photometric data is then refit once for each GPR posterior sample, with each microlensing curve applied to the best-fit model as a microlensing propagation effect (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as2-3\">2.3</a>). The flux calculated by the model is affected by the microlensing propagation effect, and otherwise the same model parameters that are measured in step 1 are allowed to vary.</div></li></ul><p>\n</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjab164af4\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjab164af4\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/876/2/107/Full/apjab164af4_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 4.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 4.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/876/2/107/Full/apjab164af4_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 4.</strong>\u00a0Red points with error bars are the residuals between an observed (simulated) SN light curve and a best-fit model. Light solid blue curves are the ~100 samples from the GPR posterior used to represent a diverse range of possible microlensing scenarios. The dotted\u2013dashed black curve is the net GPR-predicted microlensing curve, and the dashed green curves represent the 3<em>\u03c3</em> bounds on that prediction. The data are refit with each of the light blue posterior samples included as the assumed microlensing curve to estimate the uncertainty due to microlensing (see Section <a class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as3-1\">3.1</a>).</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjab164af4\" href=\"/0004-637X/876/2/107/downloadFigure/figure/apjab164af4\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjab164af4\" href=\"/0004-637X/876/2/107/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjab164af4\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>The result of these steps is a time delay measurement error for each microlensing scenario, <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/876/2/107/Full/apjab164aieqn4.gif\" alt=\"${\\rm{\\Delta }}t-{\\rm{\\Delta }}{t}_{i}^{\\mu }$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\rm{\\Delta }}t-{\\rm{\\Delta }}{t}_{i}^{\\mu }</script></span></span>, the combination of which defines a distribution of time delay errors due to microlensing. This distribution of microlensing errors characterizes an additional uncertainty on the time delay measurement, which is reported separately and then combined in quadrature with the model uncertainty. Including this measurement of uncertainty due to microlensing significantly improves the overall time delay uncertainty characterization, which is otherwise consistently underestimated when microlensing is ignored (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab164af5\">5</a>). While including the uncertainty due to microlensing certainly improves the overall error characterization, its deviations from Gaussian at <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/876/2/107/Full/apjab164aieqn5.gif\" alt=\"$| {{\\rm{\\Delta }}}_{\\mathrm{True}}-{{\\rm{\\Delta }}}_{\\mathrm{Measured}}| /\\sigma \\gtrsim 1.0$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">| {{\\rm{\\Delta }}}_{\\mathrm{True}}-{{\\rm{\\Delta }}}_{\\mathrm{Measured}}| /\\sigma \\gtrsim 1.0</script></span></span> arise from extreme cases of microlensing. The agreement could likely be improved by increasing the number of draws from the microlensing GPR posterior, which was limited due to computation time to 100 per SN, or by combining the fitting methods described in Sections <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as3-2\">3.2</a> and <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as3-4\">3.4</a> (see Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as4\">4</a>).</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjab164af5\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjab164af5\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/876/2/107/Full/apjab164af5_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 5.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 5.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/876/2/107/Full/apjab164af5_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 5.</strong>\u00a0Using the simulated SNe observed before-peak in Section <a class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as2-4\">2.4</a>, we seek to characterize the improvement on the time delay uncertainty reported by SNTD using the microlensing fitting technique described in Section <a class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as3-1\">3.1</a>. The 1000 glSNe are fit with the technique described in Section <a class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as3-2\">3.2</a>, and then we restrict the SNe to those with a <em>\u03c7</em><sup>2</sup> less than or equal to one corresponding to a P-value of 0.05. This subset represents SNe for which an observer would have a sufficiently accurate fit to believe their measurement result, but that have an unknown effect from microlensing. A value <em>n</em> on the <em>x</em>-axis corresponds to the fraction of measurements that were <em>n\u03c3</em> from the actual time delay, where <em>\u03c3</em> is the total uncertainty. The three curves represent the cumulative densities for the following distributions: the red dashed curve includes only the model uncertainty, the blue dashed\u2013dotted curve includes the model uncertainty and the measured uncertainty due to microlensing, and the green solid curve corresponds to a perfectly Gaussian characterization of the uncertainty.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjab164af5\" href=\"/0004-637X/876/2/107/downloadFigure/figure/apjab164af5\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjab164af5\" href=\"/0004-637X/876/2/107/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjab164af5\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div><h3 id=\"apjab164as3-2\" name=\"apjab164as3-2\">3.2.\u00a0Parallel Analysis</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>If the separate light curves of a multiply imaged SN are each reasonably well-sampled, then SNTD is capable of making time delay measurements by fitting each of the light curves in parallel. The parameters for a given model are separated into those that are inherent to the SN and therefore constant across each image (i.e., redshift, light-curve shape parameters, host galaxy extinction, etc.), and those that are impacted by lensing and therefore will be unique to each image (i.e., amplitude, time of peak, etc.). These parameter sets are denoted <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/876/2/107/Full/apjab164aieqn6.gif\" alt=\"${{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{\\mathrm{SN}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{\\mathrm{SN}}</script></span></span> and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/876/2/107/Full/apjab164aieqn7.gif\" alt=\"${{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{{\\rm{L}}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{{\\rm{L}}}</script></span></span>, respectively.</p><p>We use the method of nested sampling to efficiently sample the full parameter space for <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/876/2/107/Full/apjab164aieqn8.gif\" alt=\"${{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{\\mathrm{SN}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{\\mathrm{SN}}</script></span></span> and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/876/2/107/Full/apjab164aieqn9.gif\" alt=\"${{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{{\\rm{L}}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{{\\rm{L}}}</script></span></span>. Nested sampling is effectively a Markov Chain Monte Carlo (MCMC) simulation that provides an estimate of the posterior probability distribution, but nested sampling additionally allows one to calculate the integral of the distribution and is more robust for finding global maxima (Skilling <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib51\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib51\">2004</a>). SNCosmo contains a modified nested sampling algorithm, which SNTD uses to identify a best-fit model for each separate image. The default is to employ uniform prior distributions, but any form for the prior probability could be used. The algorithm takes the best-fit models identified separately, obtains a joint posterior for parameters in <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/876/2/107/Full/apjab164aieqn10.gif\" alt=\"${{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{\\mathrm{SN}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{\\mathrm{SN}}</script></span></span>, and marginalizes over the parameters in <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/876/2/107/Full/apjab164aieqn11.gif\" alt=\"${{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{{\\rm{L}}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{{\\rm{L}}}</script></span></span>. The result is a model for each image, where the parameters in <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/876/2/107/Full/apjab164aieqn12.gif\" alt=\"${{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{\\mathrm{SN}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{\\mathrm{SN}}</script></span></span> are the same for each image and the parameters in <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/876/2/107/Full/apjab164aieqn13.gif\" alt=\"${{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{{\\rm{L}}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{{\\rm{L}}}</script></span></span> are likely different. Particularly when fitting light curves with little information, this gives some constraints on the <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/876/2/107/Full/apjab164aieqn14.gif\" alt=\"${{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{\\mathrm{SN}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{\\mathrm{SN}}</script></span></span> parameters by using the fact that the underlying light curve for each image must be the same, so any observed differences are only from extrinsic lensing effects like magnification, microlensing, and a relative time delay.</p><p>Once the best-fit light-curve parameters are identified, each image is fit once more with the <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/876/2/107/Full/apjab164aieqn15.gif\" alt=\"${{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{\\mathrm{SN}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{\\mathrm{SN}}</script></span></span> parameters varying in a tightly bound range around the values measured above, while the <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/876/2/107/Full/apjab164aieqn16.gif\" alt=\"${{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{{\\rm{L}}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{{\\rm{L}}}</script></span></span> parameters remain unrestricted. This results in fine-tuned fits to each individual image (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab164af6\">6</a>).</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjab164af6\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjab164af6\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/876/2/107/Full/apjab164af6_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 6.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 6.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/876/2/107/Full/apjab164af6_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 6.</strong>\u00a0Example of measuring the time delay for a simulated glSN Ia from Section <a class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as2-4\">2.4</a>, using the \"parallel\" fitting method outlined in this section. Each panel contains one of the images of this doubly imaged SN, and the solid black line is the best-fit model. As is described in this section, the two light curves are fit in parallel, and then we obtain a joint posterior for parameters intrinsic to the SN light curve (<em>\u03b8</em><sub>SN</sub>) while marginalizing over extrinsic parameters (<em>\u03b8</em><sub>L</sub>).</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjab164af6\" href=\"/0004-637X/876/2/107/downloadFigure/figure/apjab164af6\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjab164af6\" href=\"/0004-637X/876/2/107/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjab164af6\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div><h3 id=\"apjab164as3-3\" name=\"apjab164as3-3\">3.3.\u00a0Series Analysis</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>SNTD contains a second method for determining time delays that is particularly useful in the cases of relatively sparse light curves. This method differs from that of parallel fitting in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as3-2\">3.2</a> in that the intrinsic light-curve shape is identified first by fitting all or a subset of the light curves, which is then used to measure the time delays of the multiple images simultaneously. In both cases, we utilize the knowledge that the underlying light curve is the same for every image, but in this case, we apply that leverage more directly by employing only a single set of intrinsic SN light-curve parameters <em>\u03b8</em><sub>SN</sub> (see Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as3-2\">3.2</a>) for the light-curve model, in order to obtain simultaneous constraints on the relative time delays and magnifications for every image.</p><p>To perform the series time delay measurement, SNTD effectively employs a double layered MCMC simulation that instead uses nested sampling to estimate the posterior probability distributions (see Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as3-2\">3.2</a>). The algorithm varies the <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/876/2/107/Full/apjab164aieqn17.gif\" alt=\"${{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{\\mathrm{SN}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{\\mathrm{SN}}</script></span></span> parameters in the outer layer and the relative magnifications and time delays in the inner layer, attempting to simultaneously shift (time) and scale (flux) the photometric data from all images and fit it with a single varying light-curve model (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab164af7\">7</a>). As in the parallel approach, the default for this customized \"double-nested\" sampling is to use uniform priors for all parameters, though in principle any informative priors could be used.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjab164af7\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjab164af7\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/876/2/107/Full/apjab164af7_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 7.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 7.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/876/2/107/Full/apjab164af7_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 7.</strong>\u00a0Example of measuring the time delay for a simulated glSN Ia from Section <a class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as2-4\">2.4</a>, using the \"series\" measurement method outlined in this section. The left panel shows the simulated photometry for the before-peak case, and the right panel shows the simulated photometry for the after-peak case. In both panels, blue data points correspond to the leading image and red data points correspond to the trailing image. The black curves are the best-fit models, which are realizations of the intrinsic light-curve shape. As is described in this section, the two light curves are fit together with this single model, making it a valuable method for somewhat sparsely sampled observed light curves.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjab164af7\" href=\"/0004-637X/876/2/107/downloadFigure/figure/apjab164af7\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjab164af7\" href=\"/0004-637X/876/2/107/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjab164af7\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div><h3 id=\"apjab164as3-4\" name=\"apjab164as3-4\">3.4.\u00a0Color Curve Analysis</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>It has been proposed that the effect of microlensing on (at least) SNe Ia contains an achromatic period, meaning that it is not wavelength-dependent during this time (Goldstein et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab164abib16\" id=\"fnref-apjab164abib16\">2018b</a>). Therefore, color curves, which define the observed broad-band photometric color as a function of time, might be an effective way of measuring time delays and minimizing the complications of microlensing, as each filter should be affected identically. However, this method is only useful if the SN images are observed with multi-band photometry, the color curves are not featureless, and the achromatic phase is a true effect.</p><p>In cases where the SN classification is well-known, existing templates can be used to obtain expected color curves, which can then be used to constrain the functions being used to fit the data (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab164af8\">8</a>). Otherwise, we must resort to flexible functions such as splines or Chebyshev polynomials to fit the data. In either case, we can still leverage the fact that the color curve for each image of the SN should be the same, as in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as3-3\">3.3</a>. Therefore, the same \"double-nested\" sampling algorithm is employed to obtain time delays from color curves (see Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as3-3\">3.3</a>). The algorithm once again attempts to shift (time) and scale (flux) the color curve data for all the SN images, while fitting the combined data with a single varying color curve model. SNTD allows the color curve flux data to be individually scaled in order to account for chromatic parameters in <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/876/2/107/Full/apjab164aieqn18.gif\" alt=\"${{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{{\\rm{L}}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{{\\rm{L}}}</script></span></span> (see Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as3-2\">3.2</a>) that will affect each image differently, such as dust extinction or non-achromatic microlensing.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjab164af8\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjab164af8\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/876/2/107/Full/apjab164af8_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 8.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 8.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/876/2/107/Full/apjab164af8_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 8.</strong>\u00a0Example of measuring the time delay for a simulated glSN Ia from Section <a class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as2-4\">2.4</a>, using the \"color curve\" method outlined in this section. The left panel shows the simulated photometry for the before-peak case, and the right panel shows the simulated photometry for the after-peak case. In both panels, the blue squares correspond to the leading image and the red triangles to the trailing image. The solid black curves are the best-fit color curve model, and the epoch of peak <em>B</em>-band brightness is at 60 days.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjab164af8\" href=\"/0004-637X/876/2/107/downloadFigure/figure/apjab164af8\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjab164af8\" href=\"/0004-637X/876/2/107/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjab164af8\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjab164as4\" name=\"apjab164as4\">4.\u00a0SNTD: Case Studies</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We measure the relative time delays of the 2000 simulated glSNe described in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as2-4\">2.4</a>, exploiting our knowledge of the SN classification by using SED templates to perform the light-curve fitting. The simulations are separated into SNe \"discovered\" before peak and after peak brightness. We obtained three different sets of results for each case by using the methods described in Sections <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as3-2\">3.2</a>\u2013<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as3-4\">3.4</a> to measure the time delays, which are summarized in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjab164at1\">1</a> and Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab164af9\">9</a>. All three fitting methods are measured with nearly identical accuracy and for the before-peak case, which is to be expected for these simulations as the light curves were sufficiently well-sampled to use any approach. Still, the fact that achromatic microlensing (used for these simulations, see Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as2-3\">2.3</a>) does not affect the color curve method led to a higher precision. The series fitting routine did perform slightly better in the after-peak scenario when compared to the parallel routine, reflecting that it can be more robust in cases where one or more images has a lower-quality light curve. Nevertheless, the accuracy decreased in the after-peak case study for both of these techniques, the systematic offset stemming from the lack of an inflection point to identify peak brightness, leading to an underestimation of the time delay. Likewise, the precision for each technique is reduced for the after-peak case.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjab164af9\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjab164af9\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/876/2/107/Full/apjab164af9_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 9.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 9.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/876/2/107/Full/apjab164af9_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 9.</strong>\u00a0In each panel, red corresponds to the before-peak case and blue corresponds to the after-peak case. A black dashed line marks the true time delay for each study. The mean (<em>\u03bc</em>) and standard deviation (<em>\u03c3</em>) of each distribution is shown in the upper right of each panel in corresponding colors. Panels (A)\u2013(C) show results for the parallel, series, and color curve routines, respectively, all described in Section <a class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab164as3\">3</a>.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjab164af9\" href=\"/0004-637X/876/2/107/downloadFigure/figure/apjab164af9\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\"
